   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d113977393" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Room-temperature sodium–sulfur batteries are potential candidate for sustainable large-scale energy storage systems due to their high energy density and low cost. However, the shuttling effect of high-order polysulfides (Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>n</sub>, 4&nbsp;&lt; <i>n</i> ≤ 8) usually leads to rapid capacity fading, while the reaction kinetics of low-order polysulfides (Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>n</sub>, 1 ≤ <i>n</i> ≤ 4) are slow. In this work, microporous-mesoporous carbon derived from mangosteen peels is reported as cathode materials for RT Na–S batteries. The designed micro-mesoporous structure not only effectively suppresses the shuttling effect of sodium polysulfides (NaPS), but also has high electrical conductivity and porosity, which facilitates electron/ion diffusion. Oxygen functional groups on the surface provide high catalytic activity for efficient low-order NaPS conversion. The obtained Na–S battery exhibits high reversible capacity with excellent long-term cycle performance (526.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 4 A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles) and outstanding rate performance (676.59 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 16 A g<sup>−1</sup>). This work demonstrates a novel activation strategy of biomass-derived carbons for high-performance Na–S batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202302490-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302490-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>To lower the emission of carbon dioxide and realize carbon neutrality, renewable energy has increasingly attracted the intensive interests of researchers, such as solar energy, wind energy, and water energy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0001_R_d113977385e360" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, electricity from the renewable energy is intermittent, unstable, and cannot be directly integrated into the grid.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0002_R_d113977385e365" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Environ. Sci.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is highly in demand to develop energy storage technologies with high energy density. Nowadays, conventional lithium-ion batteries have reached their limits, which is difficult to meet the ever-growing demand for energy density and safety.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0003_R_d113977385e370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Nano Adv. Funct. Mater. Nat. Commun. eScience">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a cathode material, sulfur has gradually attracted widespread attention due to its high theoretical specific capacity of 1675 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0004_R_d113977385e378" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Commun. eScience">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite the high energy density of lithium–sulfur (Li–S) batteries, the limited content and uneven geographical distribution of lithium resources make their mass production very costly.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0003_R_d113977385e383" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Nano Adv. Funct. Mater. Nat. Commun. eScience">3</a>, <a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0005_R_d113977385e383" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Commun. eScience">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The large-scale application of Li-S batteries in grid energy storage is not cost-effective. Compared with Li–S batteries, sodium–sulfur (Na–S) batteries have the advantages of abundant sodium resources, wide distribution, and low acquisition cost, which are more suitable for large-scale applications in grid energy storage.</p>
         
         <p>Although high-temperature (HT) Na–S batteries have been commercialized, there are serious safety concerns due to the operating temperature of ≈300&nbsp;°C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0006_R_d113977385e391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Nano Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Sci.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the widespread application of HT Na–S batteries has also been hindered by the problems, such as high maintenance costs, low theoretical capacity (558 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), and large energy loss.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0004_R_d113977385e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Commun. eScience">4</a>, <a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0007_R_d113977385e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Relatively speaking, room-temperature (RT) Na–S batteries are safer, cheaper, and have higher theoretical capacities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0008_R_d113977385e404" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Nano Chem. Eng. J.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, there are still many challenges in the commercialization of RT Na–S batteries, especially at the sulfur cathode. Due to the poor conductivity of sulfur itself and its sulfides, the electrochemical reaction kinetics of the battery is&nbsp;particularly slow, resulting in low battery performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0009_R_d113977385e409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, during the electrode reaction, polysulfides dissolve from the cathode into the electrolyte and diffuse to the anode, i.e., the shuttling effect, resulting in a rapid capacity decay.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0010_R_d113977385e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the conversion of sulfur to sodium sulfide has a volume change of 170%.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0007_R_d113977385e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0010_R_d113977385e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0011_R_d113977385e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The huge volume change will seriously damage the integrity of the cathode material, thereby accelerating the degradation of battery.</p>
         
         <p>To address these issues, porous carbon materials are usually employed to support sulfur. This not only improves the conductivity of sulfur but also buffers the huge volume expansion, which is beneficial to maintain the integrity of cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0012_R_d113977385e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Lett. Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the shuttling effect of polysulfides can be alleviated by the physical confinement effect of the pore structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0013_R_d113977385e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Nano Small Adv. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among the porous carbon materials, biomass-derived porous carbon materials have attracted great attention due to their advantages of wide sources, low cost, and easy availability, which can further reduce the cost of batteries and achieve waste recycling. Recently, Guo et&nbsp;al. synthesized ultra-microporous coffee carbon materials using coffee grounds as a precursor, the capacity of which maintained 95% after 2000 cycles at 1 C due to directly avoiding the formation of polysulfides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0013_R_d113977385e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Nano Small Adv. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Carter et&nbsp;al. used sucrose-derived porous carbon materials as sulfur carriers to alleviate the shuttling effect of polysulfides and achieved a capacity of 300&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles at 1 C (1.675 A g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0014_R_d113977385e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Lett.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite the great progress, high sulfur content (&gt;40 wt.%) and high-rate capability (&gt;5C) are still challengeable for biomass-derived carbon materials.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, micro-mesoporous carbon with oxygen-containing functional groups (MMPC) has been successfully synthesized by using mangosteen peels as raw materials and a unique multi-step annealing process. The unique pore structure with abundant slit pores and cylindrical pores are beneficial for the high loading of sulfur (50%) and effectively confines the shuttle effecting of polysulfides during the electrochemical process. The as-assembled RT Na–S battery with sulfur-loaded MMPC (S@MMPC) delivers a capacity of 676.59 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the high rate of 16 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The initial discharge capacity is 1404.39 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and after 1000 cycles, the battery still maintains a capacity of 526.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 4 A g<sup>−1</sup>, with a capacity retention of 58.8% (based on the capacity after the first-cycle activation). The synergistic effect of micro-mesoporous structure and surface oxygen-containing functional groups inhibits the formation of high-order polysulfides and promotes the kinetic process of low-order polysulfides, thus alleviating the polysulfide shuttling effect. Furthermore, the diffusion rate of Na<sup>+</sup> in MMPC is also greatly enhanced. Large pore volume effectively alleviates the stress change caused by volume expansion. This work provides a cost-effective strategy for the development of sulfur anode and is helpful for moving toward the practical application of RT Na–S batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202302490-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302490-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The preparation process of MMPC and immobilizing sulfur into carbon micro-mesopores are schematically demonstrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0001">1</a>. First, mangosteen peels were crushed into powder and dispersed into KOH solution. After freeze drying, the harvested powder was successively annealed at 200, 300, 400, and 700 °C in an Ar atmosphere for a certain time and washed with H<sub>2</sub>O to obtain the final MMPC. The four-step heat-treatment was intentionally adopted to construct the unique pore structures and oxygen-containing group. The control sample also was made by using the four-step heating method, but the calcined product was washed with HCl, which was denoted as MMPC-HCl. The control sample was heated to 700 °C in one step, but the calcined product was washed with H<sub>2</sub>O, which was denoted as MMPC-line. To prepare S@MMPC composite, commercially available sulfur powder was infused into MMPC materials by a two-step melt-diffusion method. After fully melting at 155 °C, the sulfur was infiltrated into the abundant slit pores and cylindrical pores of MMPC via decomposing from S<sub>8</sub> macromolecules with orthorhombic phase into metastable S<sub>2-4</sub> molecules with monoclinic phase at 200 °C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0013_R_d113977385e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Nano Small Adv. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The small S<sub>2-4</sub> molecules would effectively alleviate the formation of polysulfides and boost the reaction kinetics during the electrochemical reaction process.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302490-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1893642f-c4a9-4bd8-87b7-3110e538b7d2/aenm202302490-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1893642f-c4a9-4bd8-87b7-3110e538b7d2/aenm202302490-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c8f6e711-dfb6-4c99-ac54-72731eaec46f/aenm202302490-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1893642f-c4a9-4bd8-87b7-3110e538b7d2/aenm202302490-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302490-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302490" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram of the synthesis procedure for the micro-mesoporous carbon and sulfur composite with small sulfur molecules confined in the carbon micropores.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The morphology of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, and MMPC-line was investigated by field emission&nbsp;scanning electron microscpe (FESEM) and transmission electron microscope&nbsp;(TEM),&nbsp;as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0002">2</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). It can be seen that MMPC has a three-dimensional (3D) porous structure with obvious pores at its surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0002">2a</a>). In contrast, although MMPC-HCl still has a 3D porous structure, its surface is obviously etched, indicating that HCl has passivated the surface of MMPC-HCl (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a> (Supporting Information), although no passivation occurred at the MMPC-line surface, its structure is fragmented and its integrity cannot be maintained, indicating that direct heating to 700 °C for calcination destroyed the structural integrity of MMPC-line. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0002">2b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c</a>,<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">d</a> (Supporting Information) show TEM images of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, and MMPC-line. It can be seen from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0002">2b</a> that the MMPC maintains a stable pore structure with various pores interconnected, and there are no traces of corrosion at the boundaries. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c</a> (Supporting Information) shows that the edges of MMPC-HCl are irregular, accompanied by collapsed internal pore structure. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1d</a> (Supporting Information), the MMPC-line no longer has a 3D porous structure. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0002">2c</a> shows the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image and selected-area electron diffraction pattern (SAED, inset) of MMPC, indicating that MMPC is an amorphous carbon without an obvious crystal structure. The energy dipsersive spectroscopy (EDS) results of MMPC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) confirm the homogeneous distribution of C, O, N, and S elements. After sulfur is loaded into the MMPC, its morphology does not change significantly, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0002">2d</a>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0002">2e</a>, tiny S nanoparticles can be observed at the edges of pores. Furthermore, crystal facet (111) of sulfur with the lattice spacing of 0.39&nbsp;nm is observed in S@MMPC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0002">2f</a>), and S@MMPC exhibits a polycrystalline state due to sulfur infiltration. This shows that sulfur has successfully entered the MMPC. From the EDS spectra of S@MMPC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0002">2g</a>), it can be seen that sulfur is evenly distributed in MMPC without obvious agglomeration.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302490-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a6c5af95-fcca-41f8-85b7-55d1677d5add/aenm202302490-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a6c5af95-fcca-41f8-85b7-55d1677d5add/aenm202302490-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/93a988c4-f1e9-494f-8bdf-06b02331b942/aenm202302490-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a6c5af95-fcca-41f8-85b7-55d1677d5add/aenm202302490-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302490-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302490" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electron microscopy images. a) FESEM, b) TEM, c) HRTEM images of MMPC. Inset in (c) is the corresponding SAED pattern. d) FESEM, e) TEM, f) HRTEM images of S@MMPC. Inset in (f) is the corresponding SAED pattern. g) STEM image of S@MMPC and corresponding EDS mappings of S, C, O, and N elements.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0003">3a</a> shows the XRD patterns of MMPC before and after loading sulfur (S@MMPC). The XRD pattern of MMPC has no obvious diffraction peaks, only two bulging peaks near 20° and 45°, indicating the amorphous carbon structure of MMPC, which is consistent with the HRTEM image of MMPC. The X-ray diffraction peaks of MMPC-HCl and MMPC-line are the same as those of MMPC, indicating that they are also amorphous (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a</a>, Supporting Information). XRD patterns of the initial sulfur are assigned to be orthorhombic (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3b</a>, Supporting Information). XRD pattern of S@MMPC before heating at 200 °C is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). It can be seen from the figure that the sulfur in the orthorhombic phase is still present. The diffraction peaks of crystalline sulfur are observed in the XRD pattern of S@MMPC, which are assigned to the monoclinic crystal structure. This indicates that the crystal structure of sulfur is transformed from macromolecule S<sub>8</sub> to small S<sub>2-4</sub> molecules. This could be caused by the confinement of the unique pore structure of MMPC, where slit pores and cylindrical pores induce the access and infiltration of small sulfur molecules.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302490-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c597359d-f26f-46e3-b986-9a3f3a6b4310/aenm202302490-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c597359d-f26f-46e3-b986-9a3f3a6b4310/aenm202302490-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/50d837b5-4788-44a4-a9b5-7a95e6956503/aenm202302490-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c597359d-f26f-46e3-b986-9a3f3a6b4310/aenm202302490-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302490-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302490" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterizations of samples. a) XRD patterns of MMPC and S@MMPC. b) FTIR spectra of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC. c) Enlarged view of the rectangular area in (b). d) Raman spectra of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, MMPC-line, S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line. e) Nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, MMPC-line, and S@MMPC. f) Pore-size distribution of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, and MMPC-line. g) XPS spectra of O 1s for MMPC, MMPC-HCl, and MMPC-line. h) XPS spectra of C 1s for MMPC and S@MMPC. i) XPS spectra of O 1s for MMPC and S@MMPC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To investigate the effect of HCl and H<sub>2</sub>O on the surface of samples, the functional groups at the surface were measured by using Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0003">3b</a>, there are some same groups in MMPC and MMPC-HCl, i.e, C─H (631 cm<sup>−1</sup>), C─O─C (1097 cm<sup>−1</sup>), C─C (1583&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) and CH<sub>2</sub> (2919 cm<sup>−1</sup>). In the range of 3050–3750&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, there are four O─H (3256, 3451, 3512, and 3577 cm<sup>−1</sup>) groups at the surface of MMPC, while the surface of MMPC-HCl only contains a ─OH group at 3450 cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0003">3c</a>). This demonstrates that HCl destroys the functional groups and passivates the material surface, but H<sub>2</sub>O cannot, which may be due to the reaction of H<sup>+</sup> in HCl with the O─H group at the material surface. After the infiltration of sulfur into MMPC, S@MMPC also only has a ─OH group in the range of 3050–3750 cm<sup>−1</sup>, indicating that sulfur interacts with the groups at the surface of MMPC and changes the surface property of MMPC.</p>
         
         <p>In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0003">3d</a>, Raman spectra of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, MMPC-line, S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line are compared. The Raman spectra of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, and MMPC-line are almost the same, with D band and G band appearing ≈1341 and 1591 cm<sup>−1</sup> respectively. By calculation, the <i>I</i><sub>(D)</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>(G)</sub> values of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, and MMPC-line are 0.95, 0.97, and 0.97, respectively, indicating that MMPC has a higher degree of graphitization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0011_R_d113977385e690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In general, the higher graphitization of the material is, the better of the conductivity. So MMPC has better conductivity than MMPC-HCl and MMPC-line. After sulfur loading, the <i>I</i><sub>(D)</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>(G)</sub> value of S@MMPC, S@MPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line is 0.98, 1, and 0.99, respectively. S@MMPC still has a higher degree of graphitization, that is, better conductivity.</p>
         
         <p>The pore structures of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, and MMPC-line are studied by nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0003">3e</a> shows the typical type I isotherms of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, and MMPC-line with type H4 hysteresis loops, indicating the presence of micropores and mesopores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0015_R_d113977385e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Small Methods ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The specific surface area (SSA) of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, and MMPC-line calculated by the Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) method is 1273.36, 1065.98, and 746.95 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> respectively. After infiltration of sulfur, the SSA of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line becomes 20.93, 13.323, and 14.939 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> respectively, indicating sulfur almost fully fills the pores of the material. The quenched solid density functional theory (QSDFT) model was used to analyze the pore diameter, pore type, and pore volume of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, and MMPC-line. The chosen model was N<sub>2</sub> on carbon at 77 K (slit/cylindrical pores, QSDFT adsorption branch). The fitting results of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, and MMPC-line agree well with the measured data with the fitting error of 0.138%, 0.076%, and 0.171%, respectively, which are the smallest among all the fitted models (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information). The pore size distribution and cumulative pore volume distribution calculated by the QSDFT method are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0003">3f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a> (Supporting Information). The dominant pore size distribution is ≈0.567, 0.753, and 4.077&nbsp;nm, which indicates that MMPC holds abundant micro-mesoporous pores. The micropores of MMPC can restrict the dissolution of polysulfides from the cathode into the electrolyte solution, while the mesopores can accelerate the mass transfer process. The pore size mainly distributes ≈0.567, 0.723, and 4.077&nbsp;nm for MPPC-HCl, and 0.545, 0.753, and 4.077&nbsp;nm for MPPC-line, respectively. The micropore volume, mesopore volume, and total pore volume of MMPC are 0.433, 0.549, and 0.982 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are higher than those of MMPC-HCl (0.364, 0.444, and 0.808 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) and MMPC-line (0.254, 0.293, and 0.547 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>). The cumulative pore volume distribution curve also shows that the pore volume of MMPC is always higher than that of MMPC-HCl and MMPC-line with the increase of pore diameter. Therefore, it can be inferred that when the same amount of sulfur is loaded, MMPC alleviates the stress change caused by volume expansion, better than MMPC-HCl and MMPC-line, which is beneficial to maintaining the integrity of material microstructure and reduces the loss of sulfur. In addition, it also shows that treatment with HCl solution and direct calcination at 700&nbsp;°C destroys the material structure and reduces the pore volume. After sulfur loading, the pore size distribution of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line changes. The pore size of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line is ≈2.81, 2.808, and 3.133&nbsp;nm, respectively. This is mainly due to sulfur penetrating into the pores of the material (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was used to determine the elemental composition as well as the chemical states of elements in the samples. As shown in the survey spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), MMPC, MMPC-HCl, MMPC-line, and S@MMPC show elemental signals of C, N, O, and S, indicating the presence of these elements. In the C 1s XPS spectrum of MMPC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a>, Supporting Information), three peaks at 284.75, 285.9, and 288.75&nbsp;eV correspond to C─C, C─O, and C═O, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0016_R_d113977385e758" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The C─O and C═O peaks of MMPC shift negatively to the lower binding energy relative to MMPC-HCl and MMPC-line. In the N 1s spectrum of MMPC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>, Supporting Information), three peaks at 400.15, 402.8, and 405.65&nbsp;eV correspond to pyrrolic N, graphitic N, and NO<sub>2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0013_R_d113977385e769" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Nano Small Adv. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, there is only pyridinic N in the N 1s spectrum of MMPC-HCl. This may be caused by the reaction of HCl with nitrogen-containing groups at the surface of the material. Three peaks in the O 1s spectrum of MMPC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0003">3g</a>) are assigned to –SO<sub>2</sub>- (531.7&nbsp;eV), C─O (533.4&nbsp;eV), and O─H (536&nbsp;eV), respectively. Compared to MMPC, the –SO<sub>2</sub>- and C-O peaks of MMPC-HCl shift toward higher binding energy by 0.3 and 0.15&nbsp;eV, respectively. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c</a> (Supporting Information) shows the S 2p spectra of MMPC, MMPC-HCl, and MMPC-line. There are S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (164&nbsp;eV), S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (165.2&nbsp;eV), C-S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (164.6&nbsp;eV), C-S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (165.75&nbsp;eV), sulfate 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (168.5&nbsp;eV), sulfate 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (169.85&nbsp;eV) in the spectrum of MMPC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0017_R_d113977385e798" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the S 2p spectrum of MMPC-HCl, C-S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> has a higher binding energy (164.95&nbsp;eV) and sulfate 2p<sub>3/2</sub> has a lower binding energy (168.1&nbsp;eV) than MMPC. But, the XPS results of MMPC-line and MMPC are almost the same. This indicates that HCl significantly affects the chemical states of the surface of MMPC, thereby affecting the interaction between MMPC and sulfur when sulfur is infiltrated into MMPC. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0003">3h</a> shows the comparison of C 1s spectrum of MMPC before and after loading sulfur. The binding energies of C─O and C═O reduce after loading S. The pyrrolic N of MMPC disappears after loading S, while the pyridinic N appears (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0003">3i</a> depicts O 1s spectra of MMPC and S@MMPC. It can be seen that the binding energy of –SO<sub>2</sub>- bond in S@MMPC obviously increases, and the binding energy of C─O bond also increases. At the same time, the O─H bond disappears, and the ─CO─ bond appears. After the incorporation of sulfur into MMPC, the binding energy of C-S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> of S@MMPC decreases, while that of sulfate 2p<sub>3/2</sub> increases (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information). Theses results prove that the functional groups interact with the sulfur at the surface of MMPC and promote the transformation of macromolecule S<sub>8</sub> to small molecule S<sub>2-4</sub>. This would avoid the formation of subsequent higher-order polysulfides and simultaneously anchor the polysulfides via the O─Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub> bond.</p>
         
         <p>The elemental content of all samples was measured using an elemental analyzer (EA), as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). It can be determined that the sample contains C, N, S, and O besides H. The content difference of each element in MMPC-HCl and MMPC is small. The content of C in the MMPC-line reduces by 3&nbsp;wt.%, and the content of oxygen increases by 3&nbsp;wt.%. The S content in all three samples is 52&nbsp;wt.%. The mass fractions of S in different samples were also measured by thermogravimetric analysis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), which are 54, 54, and 52&nbsp;wt.% for the S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line samples, respectively. The sulfur in the mesopores would sublimate at relatively low temperature of 160–280 °C, and the sulfur proportion was ≈30&nbsp;wt.%. Then, the sulfur confined in the micropores (≈24&nbsp;wt.%) would evaporate at the temperature between 280 and 550 °C.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line as cathode materials in RT Na–S was evaluated by assembling coin cells. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information) shows the cyclic voltammetry curves of three samples at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.5–2.9&nbsp;V. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a> (Supporting Information), the first cathodic process (sodiation) of S@MMPC exhibits a strong peak at 0.76&nbsp;V, which is higher than the potential of S@MMPC-HCl (0.65&nbsp;V), S@MMPC-line (0.613&nbsp;V) and those reported in previous literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0013_R_d113977385e856" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Nano Small Adv. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0018_R_d113977385e856" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. ChemElectroChem J. Electroanal. Chem. Adv. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This indicates that MMPC lowers the polarization potential of S reduction and promotes S transformation. As shown in the inset of Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a> (Supporting Information), S@MMPC also presents a weak peak at 2.16&nbsp;V, which represents the transformation of S<sub>8</sub>. In the subsequent cycles, three consecutive peaks appear at 1.041, 1.229, and 1.62&nbsp;V, respectively, indicating that the sulfur undergoes three-state changes in the cathode process after the battery reaction being stabilized, namely Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0005_R_d113977385e878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Commun. eScience">5</a>, <a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0018_R_d113977385e878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. ChemElectroChem J. Electroanal. Chem. Adv. Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0019_R_d113977385e878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Sustainable Syst.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The third cyclic voltammetry curves of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0004">4a</a>. After three cycles, the peaks at ≈1.041 and 1.62&nbsp;V of S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line gradually disappear, while S@MMPC still shows three obvious peaks, which indicates that S@MMPC can remain relatively good reactivity. In addition, the cathodic onset potential of S@MMPC is 1.94&nbsp;V, higher than that of S@MMPC-HCl, S@MMPC-line, and most values in the reported literature. During the anodic process, S@MMPC shows two peaks at 1.852 and 2.122&nbsp;V, which are attributed to the transformation of Na<sub>2</sub>S to S<sub>4</sub>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0004">4b</a>, the S@MMPC electrode releases a specific capacity of 1404.39 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> for the first cycle, and still maintains 507.42 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1700 cycles. S@MMPC-HCl only retains the specific capacity of 91.48 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 700 cycles, while the specific capacity of S@MMPC-line after 850 cycles is 128.22 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information) shows Coulombic efficiencies (CE) for the three samples. The Coulombic efficiency of S@MMPC remains 99% for 1700 cycles, while the Coulombic efficiency of S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line fluctuates greatly. The capacity retention of S@MMPC electrode is 52.69% (based on the 2nd discharge capacity), higher than that of S@MMPC-HCl (8.96%) and S@MMPC-line (14.64%). The capacity decay rate per cycle is 0.0278% for S@MMPC, much lower than that of S@MMPC-HCl (0.1302%) and S@MMPC-line (0.1005%). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information) shows the charge–discharge curves for the 1st, 2nd, 500th, and last cycle of the three samples in the voltage range of 0.5–2.9&nbsp;V at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The first discharge curve appears as a plateau near 0.76&nbsp;V, and the subsequent discharge curves show three inclined voltage plateaus in the voltage range of 0.7–2&nbsp;V, consistent with the results of CV curves.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302490-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c7ddbe33-0c1b-46f7-a6bd-ebe39c0e17dc/aenm202302490-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c7ddbe33-0c1b-46f7-a6bd-ebe39c0e17dc/aenm202302490-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1aaad580-df6f-4df6-b719-c916a6d4b5b2/aenm202302490-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c7ddbe33-0c1b-46f7-a6bd-ebe39c0e17dc/aenm202302490-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302490-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302490" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">RT-Na/S batteries performance. a) CV curves of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line. b) Cyclability at a low current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Rate capabilities at various current densities (0.5, 1, 2, 4, 8, 16 A g<sup>−1</sup>). d) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of S@MMPC at 0.5, 1, 2, 4, 8, 16 A g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Percentage of capacity retention at 1, 2, 4, 8, 16 A g<sup>−1</sup> for S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line, relative to their respective capacity at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. f) Comparison of the specific capacity versus current density for S@MMPC and the reported Na–S cathodes from the literature. g) Cyclability of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line at 4 A g<sup>−1</sup> (initially activated at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>). h) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line at 4 A g<sup>−1</sup>. i) Cycling performance and the corresponding Coulombic efficiency of the thick S@MMPC electrode with mass loading of 4.06&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further study the rate performance, the S@MMPC electrodes are charged/discharged at different current densities from 0.5 to 16 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0004">4c,d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information), the average specific capacity of the S@MMPC electrode is 1104.39, 1017.22, 938.79, 867.14, 791.88, and 676.59&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5, 1, 2, 4, 8, and 16 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which is much better than that of S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line. It is worth noting that the reversible capacity of 1045.45 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be recovered when the current density is restored to 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating the high reversibility of the electrode. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information) shows that S@MMPC has a better Coulombic efficiency. The excellent rate capability of the S@MMPC electrode can be attributed to its unique micro-meso pore structure, which facilitates the penetration of electrolyte and confine small sulfur molecules in the micropores. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0004">4e</a> plots the capacity retention ratio of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line at 1, 2, 4, 8, and 16 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The results show that S@MMPC not only retains a higher reversible capacity but also has a higher capacity retention rate at different rates. For S@MMPC, the capacity retentions are 92.1%, 85%, 78.5%, 71.7%, and 61.3% at 1, 2, 4, 8, and 16 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. For S@MMPC-HCl, these values are 90.5%, 80.8%, 71.8%, 61%, and 43.6%, respectively. For S@MMPC-line, these values are 89.6%, 76.9%, 64.7%, 48%, and 17%, respectively. The rate performance of the S@MMPC electrode is compared with the state-of-the-art sulfur-carbon cathodes in the literature in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0004">4f</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). It can be observed that the rate capability of S@MMPC is competitive. To rule out the capacity contribution of the S carrier, the performance of bare MMPC was also tested against Na foil between 0.5 and 2.9&nbsp;V. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information), the sulfur-free MMPC shows negligible reversible capacity. In order to study the influence of InI<sub>3</sub> on battery performance, comparative experiments were conducted. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a</a> (Supporting Information), InI<sub>3</sub> has insignificant influence on the battery rate performance. Only when the current density is back to 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the battery with InI<sub>3</sub> shows higher capacity. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17b</a> (Supporting Information), the capacity fading of the battery containing InI<sub>3</sub> is slower than that of the battery without InI<sub>3</sub>, and the capacity fading rates per cycle being 0.0655% and 0.0846%, respectively. This suggests that InI<sub>3</sub> can improve the stability of battery cycling, which is consistent with the reported literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0003_R_d113977385e1019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Nano Adv. Funct. Mater. Nat. Commun. eScience">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Remarkably, the S@MMPC electrode also exhibits excellent cycling stability even at high current densities. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0004">4g</a>, the S@MMPC electrode maintains the specific capacity of 526.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 4 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which is higher than that of S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line. The Coulombic efficiency of S@MMPC remains stable, always above 99% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). The capacity retention of S@MMPC electrode is 58.8% (based on the 2nd discharge capacity), higher than that of S@MMPC-HCl (46.87%) and S@MMPC-line (20.17%). The capacity decay rate per cycle is 0.0412% for S@MMPC, much lower than that of S@MMPC-HCl (0.0532%) and S@MMPC-line (0.0799%). During the long cycling process, the battery capacity fluctuates probably due to the temperature effect. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0004">4h</a> shows the charge–discharge curves of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line at 4 A g<sup>−1</sup>. It can be seen that the polarization potential difference of S@MMPC is lower than that of S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line, indicating that MMPC can activate sulfur molecules, lower the redox barrier between sulfur and sodium sulfide, and accelerate the reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0020_R_d113977385e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nano Energy">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The mass loading of active materials is also an important index to evaluate battery performance. The mass loading of active material in the S@MMPC electrode is increased to 4.06&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, and a high reversible capacity of 636.95&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> still maintains after 200 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0004">4i</a>. The long cycling stability of S@MMPC suggests that micropore confinement and surface group modification may be effective strategies to develop highly stable cathode materials for RT Na–S batteries. Along with the formation of polysulfides and the shuttling effect, rapid self-discharge is a serious problem for Na–S batteries. Therefore, the self-discharge of S@MMPC electrodes was further investigated for RT Na–S batteries. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information) shows the open circuit voltage (OCV) of the S@MMPC cell versus time over 60 days. Surprisingly, the OCV remains above 2.1&nbsp;V for 60 days, and the voltage only drops from 2.52 to 2.27&nbsp;V within 60 days, which indicates the low self-discharge of the S@MMPC cell.</p>
         
         <p>Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) of the three electrodes before and after rate cycling are analyzed over the frequency range from 0.1&nbsp;Hz to 1&nbsp;MHz with an amplitude of 5&nbsp;mV. In the Nyquist plots in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0005">5a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information), the intercept on the <i>Z</i><sub>Re</sub> axis in the high-frequency region is the Ohmic resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ohm</sub>), which is related to the combined resistance of the electrode assembly and electrolyte. The semicircle represents the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), where <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> is at the higher frequency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0021_R_d113977385e1085" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Electrochim. Acta">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The 45° line in the low-frequency region represents the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>), which is related to the solid-state diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrode material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0022_R_d113977385e1096" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. ACS Nano">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the charged state before rate cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a</a>, Supporting Information), the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of S@MMPC is 644.9 Ω, and the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line is 697.9 and 790.2&nbsp;Ω, respectively. In the charged state after rate cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20b</a>,<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">c</a>, Supporting Information), these values decrease significantly to 58.95, 69.26, and 74.08 Ω, respectively. The more significant difference lies in discharged after rate cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0005">5a</a>). The S@MMPC electrode exhibits a lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value (86.67 Ω) than S@MMPC-HCl (239.5 Ω) and S@MMPC-line (180.2 Ω). According to previous reports, the phase angle in the low-frequency region (below 1&nbsp;Hz) of the Bode plots can reflect the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion ability. The smaller the phase angle is, the faster the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion ability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0023_R_d113977385e1128" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys. Nano Energy Interdiscip. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0005">5b</a>, S@MMPC exhibits the smallest phase angle among three samples in the low frequency region, indicating faster ion diffusion kinetics than other samples. The diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrode can be obtained through the curve in the low frequency region according to <i>Z</i><sub>Re</sub> = <i>R</i><sub>ohm</sub>+<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>+<i>σω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> and <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> = <i>R</i><sup>2</sup><i>T</i><sup>2</sup>/(2<i>A</i><sup>2</sup><i>n</i><sup>4</sup><i>F</i><sup>4</sup><i>C</i><sub>Na+</sub><sup>2</sup><i>σ</i><sup>2</sup>), where <i>D</i> represents the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient, <i>R</i> represents the gas constant, <i>T</i> is the absolute temperature, <i>A</i> is the surface area (here, <i>A</i> = 1.13&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>), <i>n</i> is the number of transferred electrons, <i>F</i> is Faraday's constant, <i>C</i><sub>Na+</sub> is the concentration, <i>σ</i> is the Warburg factor, and <i>ω</i> is angular frequency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0024_R_d113977385e1201" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Electrochem. Commun.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0005">5c</a> demonstrates that the S@MMPC electrode exhibits the smallest σ among these samples, indicating the largest Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (1.35×10<sup>−17</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>). In contrast, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient in S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line is calculated to be 3.36×10<sup>−18</sup> and 2.49×10<sup>−18</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The exchange current density <i>i</i><sub>0</sub> for sulfur redox is calculated according to the Butler-Vollmer equation <i>i</i><sub>0</sub> = <i>RT</i>/(<i>nFAR</i><sub>ct</sub>), where <i>R</i> is the gas constant, <i>T</i> is the absolute temperature, <i>n</i> is the number of transferred electrons, <i>F</i> is Faraday's constant, and <i>A</i> is the surface area (here, <i>A</i> = 1.13&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0025_R_d113977385e1256" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The exchange current density of S@MMPC is calculated to be 0.13&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, much larger than that of S@MMPC-HCl (0.05&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>) and S@MMPC-line (0.06&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>), respectively. The fast redox kinetics is favorable for the improved rate capability and lowered decay rate per cycle.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302490-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/63189a06-5468-4629-80fa-984fbd501613/aenm202302490-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/63189a06-5468-4629-80fa-984fbd501613/aenm202302490-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1827ba82-b871-4047-b998-e8795443922a/aenm202302490-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/63189a06-5468-4629-80fa-984fbd501613/aenm202302490-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302490-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302490" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Kinetic investigation. a) EIS Nyquist plots and b) Bode plots of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line after the measurement of rate performance (discharged state). c) The relationship between <i>Z</i><sub>Re</sub> and <i>ω<sup>−</sup></i><sup>1/2</sup> in the low frequency region. The inset is Na-ion diffusion coefficients of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line calculated from EIS. d) GITT curves of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line. e) Peak current versus square root of scan rates. f) Na-ion diffusion coefficients of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line calculated from CV curves of different scan rates. g,h) Double logarithmic plots of peak current versus scan rate for peak 1 and peak 2. i) Adsorption-limited contribution comparison at various scan rates.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was performed on S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line electrodes, with 0.5&nbsp;h current pulse of 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> followed by 4&nbsp;h relaxation. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0005">5d</a> shows the potential response curves of the three electrodes during the charge-discharge process. The S@MMPC electrode shows lower overpotential than S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line, validating the kinetically favorable sulfur redox in S@MMPC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0026_R_d113977385e1304" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Energy Lett.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The following desodation and sodation processes also validate the kinetically favorable sulfur redox in S@MMPC. The chemical diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> is calculated and shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information). Overall, the diffusion coefficient for S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line electrodes are comparable at the low current density, all in the range of 10<sup>−11</sup>–10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>. These results suggest that the kinetics enhancement is not related to solid-state diffusion effects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0007_R_d113977385e1324" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The kinetics of the S@MMPC cathode is further analyzed by scanning the CV curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information) at different scanning rates from 0.1 to 0.8&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Both the peak current and potential polarization of these three cathode materials increase with the scanning rate. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> is calculated based on the Randles–Sevick equation, <i>I</i><sub>p</sub> = 2.69×10<sup>5</sup><i>n</i><sup>1.5</sup><i>AD</i><sub>Na+</sub><sup>0.5</sup><i>C</i><sub>Na+</sub><i>v</i><sup>0.5</sup>, where <i>I</i><sub>p</sub> represents the peak current, <i>n</i> is the number of electrons in the reaction (here <i>n</i> = 2, 2Na + S → Na<sub>2</sub>S), <i>A</i> is the electrode area (here, <i>A</i> = 1.13&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>), <i>C</i><sub>Na+</sub> is the concentration, and <i>v</i> represents the scanning rate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0027_R_d113977385e1380" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From the linear relationship of <i>I</i><sub>p</sub> and <i>v</i><sup>0.5</sup> for the cathodic and anodic peaks of these three samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0005">5e</a>), <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> values are obtained. Both the cathodic (peak 1) and anodic (peak 2) <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> of S@MMPC are higher than these of S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0005">5f</a>), indicating that the pore structure and surface functional groups of MMPC effectively enhance the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, thereby improving the cycle performance.</p>
         
         <p>To further analyze the differences in kinetics, the change in current with the scanning rate can be analyzed as log <i>i</i> = log <i>a</i> + <i>b</i>∙log <i>v</i>, where the <i>b</i> value indicates the diffusion-limited behavior (when the <i>b</i> value is close to 0.5) or adsorption-limited behavior (when <i>b</i> value is close to 1) during the electrochemical reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0028_R_d113977385e1424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0005">5g,h</a>, the fitting curves are almost linear, and the calculated <i>b</i> value of peak 1 for S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line is 1.010, 0.898, and 0.890, respectively. This demonstrates that the conversion of S to Na<sub>2</sub>S in S@MMPC is an adsorption-limited process, which is a faster kinetics process than S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line. The <i>b</i> value of peak 2 for S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line is 0.796, 0.775, and 0.755, respectively, indicating that both ion diffusion and adsorption are involved in the electrochemical process. The reaction kinetics from the contribution of the adsorption-limited capacity to the total reversible capacity can be analyzed quantitatively according to <i>i</i> = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> <i>v</i> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0029_R_d113977385e1453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The parameters <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> can be determined by plotting <i>i</i>/<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>&nbsp;versus <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> at a fixed potential. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> (Supporting Information) shows that 93.85% of the total capacity is contributed by adsorption-controlled capacity for the S@MMPC electrode at a scan rate of 0.8&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Even at a low scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the calculated adsorption-limited contribution is as high as 82.75%. The adsorption-limited contribution can be further increased to 87.23% at 0.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, 90.18% at 0.4&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, and 92.61% at 0.6&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, as presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0005">5i</a>. In contrast, S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line electrodes show overall lower adsorption-limited contribution at the same scan rates. These results are consistent with the excellent rate kinetics exhibited by S@MMPC at the high current density.</p>
         
         <p>Since the separator is in close contact with the cathode, the color change of the separator during cycling reflects the formation and dissolution of sodium polysulfides from the cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202302490-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202302490-bib-0030_R_d113977385e1494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0006">6a–c</a> show the FESEM images and digital photos of the separators using S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line as cathodes after the measurement of rate performance. The separator color of S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line visibly changes from white to dark yellow after the measurement. In contrast, the separator of S@MMPC retains white, indicating the negligible dissolution of polysulfides from the S@MMPC cathode during cycling. It can be seen from the FESEM images that the surface of the S@MMPC separator is clean and free of foreign matter, while a large number of polysulfide particles grow on the surface of the S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line separators. It is well known that the better the conductivity is, the clearer the FESEM photographs taken. The FESEM images of separators from the S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line cells are clearer than the pristine separators (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information) and S@MMPC separators, due to the enhanced conductivity of the separators by the attached polysulfides. The enhanced conductivity of separator easily leads to short circuit of the battery, which also explains the better performance of the S@MMPC cell. The same phenomenon is also observed on the Na anode, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information). Dark yellow polysulfides are observed on the anodes of S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line, but not on the anode of S@MMPC cell, indicating that MMPC can greatly hinder the shuttling effect of polysulfides, thereby improving the electrochemical performance of the battery. The FESEM images of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line pristine and cycled cathodes are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0006">6d–f</a>. The pristine electrodes show a relatively compact and flat morphology. It is worth noting that the surface of the cycled S@MMPC electrode still maintains a dense and flat morphology, because MMPC can well relieve the stress caused by the volume expansion, thereby maintaining a stable electrode structure. On the surface of S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line, there are obvious cracks, indicating that the volume expansion has destroyed the structure of the electrode material. The EDS images of S@MMPC, S@MMPC-HCl, and S@MMPC-line cycled electrodes show that a layer of SEI film is formed (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>–<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information). But compared with S@MMPC-HCl and S@MMPC-line, the SEI film on the surface of S@MMPC is more stable and complete without obvious breakage, thus maintaining good cycle performance. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0006">6g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a> (Supporting Information) illustrate in detail the reaction pathways on the surface loaded with sulfur during the charging and discharging processess. When sulfur is loaded into biomass carbon, whether it is the residual S<sub>8</sub> macromolecule or the S<sub>4</sub> small molecule entering into the micropores, H<sup>+</sup> in C─O─H is removed. The exposed O can form weak O─S bond with the S atom, thereby adsorbing sulfur on the material surface, which is consistent with the XPS results shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202302490-fig-0003">3i</a>. When S@MMPC electrode starts to discharge, the weak O─S bond can be broken, and both the electronegative O and the high-valence S are easily combined with Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrolyte, thereby forming O─Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub> bonds to adsorb sodium polysulfides. The residual S<sub>8</sub> macromolecules are converted into Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>8</sub> during the first discharge, and then into Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>S in turn. During the charging process, Na<sup>+</sup> begins to detach from the cathode, but the electronegative O can still adsorb sodium polysulfides through the O─Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub> bond until the Na<sup>+</sup> is completely gone, thereby forming O─S bonds again to adsorb sulfur. Due to the existence of micropores, the final charge product is S<sub>4</sub> small molecule.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202302490-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a8820823-e80b-44f3-a81b-b1f8d8663cb1/aenm202302490-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a8820823-e80b-44f3-a81b-b1f8d8663cb1/aenm202302490-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f1645c59-c944-4470-9115-e70ad0f4e09c/aenm202302490-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a8820823-e80b-44f3-a81b-b1f8d8663cb1/aenm202302490-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202302490-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202302490" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">FESEM images of separators from the cells using a) S@MMPC, b) S@MMPC-HCl, and c) S@MMPC-line as the cathodes after rate measurement (discharged state). The inset is the corresponding digital photo. FESEM images of d) S@MMPC, e) S@MMPC-HCl, and f) S@MMPC-HCl cathodes after rate measurement (discharged state). g) Schematic diagram of the charge–discharge reaction pathway on the cathode surface of Na–S battery.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202302490-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302490-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, micro-mesoporous carbon materials with oxygen functional groups are synthesized from mangosteen peels as sulfur host for RT Na–S batteries. MMPC has excellent electrical conductivity and an unique porous structure, which effectively enhances reaction kinetics and facilitates ion transport as electrode material. The abundant micropores and mesopores of MMPC physically confine sulfur in the pores and further alleviate the volume expansion of electrode materials during cycling. More importantly, the abundant oxygen functional groups at the surface not only promote the adsorption of sulfur but also catalyze the rapid conversion process of sulfur to sodium sulfide, thus improving the electrochemical performance of RT Na–S batteries. As a result, the S@MMPC electrode delivers a high capacity of 676.59 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 16 A g<sup>−1</sup> and maintains a capacity of 526.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 4 A g<sup>−1</sup> even after 1000 cycles. This work provides a new strategy for constructing high-performance RT Na–S batteries with cost-effective biomass-derived carbon materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202302490-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202302490-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302490-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302490-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302490-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202302490-sec-0070-title"> Material Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p>Potassium hydroxide (KOH) was acs grade purchased from Aladdin Corporation. Hydrochloric acid was purchased from WUXI JINGKE CHEMICAL CO., LTD. and diluted to 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> prior to use. Deionized water was 18.2 MΩ produced from Milli-Q. Mangosteen peels were dried at 80 °C for 24&nbsp;h and ground into powder. The powder was then stirred in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> HCl overnight, washed with deionized water until the water became colorless, and dried at 80&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h. The dried mangosteen-peel powder and KOH were uniformly dispersed into water at a mass ratio of 2:1, stirred overnight, and then freeze-dried to remove water. Then the mixed precursor was heated successively at 200, 300, and 400 °C for 1.5&nbsp;h, 700 °C for 3&nbsp;h in a tube furnace at a heating rate of 3 °C min<sup>−1</sup> in an Ar atmosphere. Finally, the calcined product was washed with deionized water and vacuum-dried at 80 °C for 12&nbsp;h to obtain the final product (designated as MMPC). The mixed powder of KOH and mangosteen peel was heated successively at 200, 300, and 400 °C for 1.5&nbsp;h, 700 °C for 3&nbsp;h in a tube furnace at a heating rate of 3 °C min<sup>−1</sup> in an Ar atmosphere. The calcined product was washed with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> HCl and vacuum-dried at 80 °C for 12&nbsp;h to obtain the control sample (designated as MMPC-HCl). The mixed powder of KOH and mangosteen peels was heated at 700 °C for 3&nbsp;h in a tube furnace at a heating rate of 3 °C min<sup>−1</sup> in an Ar atmosphere. The calcined product was washed with deionized water and vacuum-dried at 80 °C for 12&nbsp;h to obtain the control sample (designated as MMPC-line).</p>
                  
                  <p>S@MMPC was obtained by loading sulfur onto MMPC by a two-step melt-diffusion method. MMPC and sulfur were uniformly ground at a mass ratio of 1:1.1 for at least 20 min, followed by being heated at 155 °C for 15&nbsp;h and then at 200&nbsp;°C for 0.5&nbsp;h in a tube furnace under an Ar atmosphere to obtain the final product (S@MMPC). The preparation process of S@MMPC-line and S@MMPC-HCl was similar to that of S@MMPC, except that MMPC was replaced by MMPC-line and MMPC-HCl, respectively.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302490-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202302490-sec-0080-title"> Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>Morphological and microstructural information of the samples were obtained using a field emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM, JSM-IT800, Japan), and transmission electron microscope (TEM, FEI Tecnai G2 F20, America). The elemental distributions were recorded using an energy-dispersive spectrometer connected to a TEM (Hitachi HF5000, Japan). The crystal structure information of samples was obtained by X-ray diffractometer (XRD, Bruker D8A A25, Germany) with a Cu Kα source (<i>λ</i> = 1.5418 Å). The chemical composition and electronic structure of the samples were analyzed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XSP, Thermo Scientific ESCALAB Xi<sup>+</sup>, America) with a monochromatic Al anode X-ray source. The elemental content of the samples was measured by an elemental analyzer (Elementar Unicube, Germany). The nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherm curves of the samples were measured by Autosorb-iQ automatic specific surface and pore size distribution analyzer (Quantachrome, America). The Quenched Solid Density Functional Theory (QSDFT) mode was chosen to analyze the pore diameter, pore type, and pore volume of the samples. The molecular structure and surface groups of samples were analyzed by Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR, Thermo Scientific Nicolet IS5, America) and Raman spectroscopy (Thermo Scientific DXR3 microscope, America) with a 532&nbsp;nm laser. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was monitored through TA SDT 650 system from 30 to 800 °C in N<sub>2</sub> with a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202302490-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202302490-sec-0090-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The active material, carbon black (Super-P), and carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) binder with the mass ratio of 8:1:1 were ground into a homogeneous slurry in deionized water, and then the slurry was coated on carbon-coated aluminum foil. Electrodes were obtained after drying in a vacuum oven at 60 °C for 12&nbsp;h. Unless otherwise stated, the mass loading of active material was ≈1&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. Coin cells were assembled in an argon-filled glove box (&lt;0.01&nbsp;ppm of water and oxygen). Glass fibers (Whatman, GF/D) were used as separators. Sodium foils (Guangdong Canrd New Energy Technology Co., Ltd.) were used as counter and reference electrodes, respectively. 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in ethylene carbonate/propylene carbonate (EC/PC, 1:1 by volume) containing 2 wt.% fluorinated ethylene carbonate (FEC) and 10&nbsp;m<span class="smallCaps">m</span> InI<sub>3</sub> additives was employed as the electrolyte. The galvanostatic measurements were performed on LANHE CT3002A battery test systems (Wuhan, China) at room temperature (26 ± 4 °C) in the voltage range of 0.5–2.9&nbsp;V. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were performed on a CHI 760E electrochemical workstation at scan rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, and 0.8&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was measured in the range of 0.1–10<sup>5</sup>&nbsp;Hz with an amplitude of 5&nbsp;mV at an open circuit voltage using a PARSTAT MC 2000A electrochemical workstation. The relationship between the scanning rate and the peak current was fitted using the modified formula log <i>i</i> = <i>b</i>∙log <i>ν</i> + <i>c</i> derived from <i>i</i> = <i>a</i>∙<i>ν<sup>b</sup></i>. The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was completed on LANHE CT3002A battery test systems (Wuhan, China). For post-cycling analysis of the batteries, the batteries were disassembled in the glove box. The cathodes and separators were soaked in EC at 45&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h, then washed three times with absolute ethanol, and soaked in absolute ethanol at room temperature for 12&nbsp;h. In this work, the specific capacity and current density were calculated based on the mass of sulfur.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
